The First to Give Good News: Multiple Slaves Bringing News of Birth
Definition and ruling of 'bishara' determining which slave is freed.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define bishara according to the Hanafi school and apply it to determine which slave is freed when multiple slaves bring news of birth.

Core Idea
Only the first joyful news counts as bishara; simultaneous news frees all.

A master promises freedom to any slave who brings him news of a birth. But if several slaves bring the same news, who gets freed? The answer depends on the legal definition of 'bishara' (good news) and whether the news is brought separately or together. This lesson unpacks the Hanafi ruling based on custom and the nature of joy.

Guided Path
1
Identify the master's statement and the condition
The master says: 'Every slave who gives me news of the birth of so-and-so is free.' This sets up a conditional reward tied to the act of bringing news of a specific event.
2
Define bishara linguistically and legally
Bishara is defined as news that is so joyful it changes the complexion of the face. The Hanafi school adds that this joyfulness is determined by custom ('urf). Only the first instance of such news truly brings joy.
3
Apply to sequential vs. simultaneous news
If slaves bring news one after another, only the first slave is freed because the joy is realized from him alone. If they bring news at exactly the same time, all are freed because the joy is realized from all of them together.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَمَنْ قَالَ كُلُّ عَبْدٍ بَشَّرَنِي بِوِلَادَةِ فُلَانَةَ فَهُوَ حُرٌّ فَبَشَّرَهُ ثَلَاثَةٌ مُتَفَرِّقِينَ عَتَقَ الْأَوَّلُ لِأَنَّ الْبَشَارَةَ اسْمٌ لِخَيْرٍ يُغَيِّرُ بَشْرَةَ الْوَجْهِ وَشَرْطُهُ كَوْنُهُ سَارّاً بِالْعُرْفِ وَهَذَا إِنَّمَا يَتَحَقَّقُ مِنَ الْأَوَّلِ وَإِنْ بَشَّرُوهُ مَعاً عَتَقُوا لِأَنَّهَا تَحَقَّقَتْ مِنَ الْكُلِّ
And whoever said, 'Every slave who gives me news of the birth of so-and-so is free,' then three slaves gave him news separately, the first is freed because 'bishara' is a name for a good thing that changes the skin of the face, and its condition is that it be joyful according to custom. And this is only realized from the first. And if they gave him news together, they are all freed because it is realized from all.
Source pages p. 107

Commentary
This passage defines the legal meaning of 'bishara' (good news). The Hanafi position is that bishara is news that brings joy, such that it changes the complexion. Customarily, only the first news is truly joyful; subsequent news does not have the same effect. Therefore, if slaves bring news separately, only the first is freed. If they bring news together, all are freed because each contributed to the joyful news. This ruling is based on the 'urf (custom) and the linguistic definition.

Key Terms
بشارة (bishāra): Good news that changes the complexion of the face due to joy; legally defined by custom.
عرف (‘urf): Customary practice; used to define what constitutes joyful news.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) All three
B) The first one
C) The last one
D) The one closest to the owner

Key Takeaway
Only the first bearer of joyful news qualifies for the reward; if all bring it together, they share the reward.

Expiation by Buying and Freeing: Specific Slave vs. One's Father
Dispute about expiation via conditional freeing of a slave, distinguishing specific slave vs. father.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school distinguishes between buying a specific slave and buying one's father for expiation of an oath, and identify the hadith that supports this distinction.

Core Idea
Buying a relative for expiation counts as manumission itself, but buying a specific slave does not because intention must connect to the oath, not the purchase.

This lesson examines a key difference in the Hanafi school regarding expiation for an oath. The passage contrasts two scenarios: buying a named slave with the intent to free as expiation, and buying one's own father. The ruling hinges on whether the purchase itself can be considered the act of manumission. We will explore the Hanafi position and the opposing views of Zufar and al-Shafi'i.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify the two scenarios: buying a specific slave (e.g., Zayd) with conditional freeing does not count as expiation; buying one's father does count.
The distinction is based on whether the purchase itself is considered the freeing act.
2
Identify the opponent and their view
Zufar and Imam al-Shafi'i hold that both scenarios are invalid because purchase and freeing are contradictory acts.
3
Follow the opponent's proof
Their dalil: purchase establishes ownership, freeing removes it; they cannot be the same act. The cause (illa) for freeing a relative is kinship, not purchase.
They see the purchase only as a condition, not the act of freeing.
4
Analyse the Hanafi response
The Hanafis cite the hadith: 'A child will never repay his parent except that he finds him a slave and buys him and frees him.' They argue that the hadith makes the purchase itself a manumission because no further act is required.
The analogy 'one who gives water and quenches' shows the act includes the result.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference: Hanafis allow expiation by buying and freeing one's father; Zufar and Shafi'i do not. This affects the validity of the expiation.
In Hanafi practice, this ruling is upheld.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَلَوْ قَالَ إِنِ اشْتَرَيْتُ فُلَانًا فَهُوَ حُرٌّ فَاشْتَرَاهُ يَنْوِي بِهِ كَفَّارَةَ يَمِينِهِ لَمْ يَجُزْهُ لِأَنَّ الشَّرْطَ قِرَانُ النِّيَّةِ بِعِلَّةِ الْعَلَقِ وَهِيَ الْيَمِينُ فَأَمَّا الشِّرَاءُ فَشَرْطُهُ وَإِنِ اشْتَرَى أَبَاهُ يَنْوِي عَنْ كَفَّارَةِ يَمِينِهِ أَجْزَأَهُ عِنْدَنَا خِلَافاً لِزُفَرَ وَالشَّافِعِيِّ لَهُمَا أَنَّ الشِّرَاءَ شَرْطُ الْعَلَقِ فَأَمَّا الْعِلَّةُ فَهِيَ الْقَرَابَةُ وَهَذَا لِأَنَّ الشِّرَاءَ إِثْبَاتُ الْمِلْكِ وَالْإِعْتَاقُ إِزَالَتُهُ وَبَيْنَهُمَا مُنَافَاةٌ وَلَنَا أَنَّ شِرَاءَ الْقَرِيبِ إِعْتَاقٌ لِقَوْلِهِ عَلَيْهِ السَّلَامُ لَنْ يُجْزِيَ وَلَدٌ وَالِدَهُ إِلَّا أَنْ يَجِدَهُ مَمْلُوكاً فَيَشْتَرِيَهُ فَيُعْتِقَهُ جَعَلَ نَفْسَ الشِّرَاءِ إِعْتَاقاً لِأَنَّهُ لَا يُشْتَرَطُ غَيْرُهُ فَصَارَ نَظِيرَ قَوْلِهِ سَقَاهُ فَأَرْوَاه
And if he said, 'If I buy so-and-so, he is free,' then he bought him intending by it the expiation of his oath, it is not valid for him because the condition is the conjunction of intention with the cause of the obligation, which is the oath. As for the purchase, it is its condition. And if he bought his father intending it for the expiation of his oath, it suffices him according to us, contrary to Zufar and al-Shafi'i. For them, the purchase is the condition of the obligation, but the cause is the kinship. And this is because purchase is the establishment of ownership, and manumission is its removal, and there is contradiction between them. And for us, the purchase of a relative is a manumission because of his statement (peace be upon him): 'A child will never repay his parent except that he finds him a slave and buys him and frees him.' He made the purchase itself a manumission because nothing else is required besides it. So it became analogous to his statement, 'He gave him water and quenched him.'
Source pages p. 107

Commentary
This passage discusses the validity of using a conditional freeing of a slave as expiation for an oath. The Hanafi position distinguishes between buying a specific person (like Zayd) and buying one's father. In the first case, the intention to expiate must be connected to the cause (the oath), but here it is connected to the condition (purchase), so it fails. However, when buying one's father, the purchase itself is considered a manumission by the hadith, so the intention to expiate is valid because the purchase and freeing are simultaneous. Zufar and al-Shafi'i object that purchase and freeing are contradictory acts, but the Hanafis reply with the hadith that makes the purchase of a relative equivalent to freeing. The analogy at the end ('one who gives water and quenches') shows that the act includes the result.

Key Terms
كفارة يمين (kaffārat yamīn): Expiation for a broken oath, which can be freeing a slave, feeding, or clothing.
علة العلق (‘illat al-‘alaq): The cause of the obligation; here it is the oath itself.
قرابة (qarāba): Kinship; the cause according to opposing scholars for the validity of freeing a relative.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, buying one's father with the intention of expiating an oath is valid because:
A) Kinship is the cause of the obligation.
B) The purchase itself is considered manumission by the hadith.
C) The intention is linked to the oath directly through the purchase.
D) It is valid only if the father is a slave and the son is the master.

Key Takeaway
When buying a slave for expiation of an oath, the intention must be linked to the cause (the oath), not the condition (the purchase); an exception exists for relatives where the purchase itself is considered manumission due to the hadith.

Ruling on Good News (Bisharah) and Emancipation of Slaves
Explains the ruling when multiple slaves give news separately or together.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on conditional manumission when multiple slaves give separate or simultaneous good news, and explain the legal reasoning based on the definition of bisharah.

Core Idea
Bisharah is only truly realized with the first bearer of joyful news; simultaneous delivery fulfills the condition for all.

This lesson examines a specific scenario from Hanafi law regarding oaths of manumission. A master declares that any slave who brings him joyful news of his wife's birth will be freed. The passage clarifies how this condition applies when multiple slaves deliver the news either separately or together, grounding the ruling in the linguistic and customary meaning of 'bisharah' (good news). We will explore the reasoning behind why only the first slave is freed in one case, yet all are freed in the other.

Guided Path
1
Identify the condition
The master's statement creates a conditional outcome: freedom for the slave who brings good news. Note the precise wording: 'Every slave who gives me the good news... is free.'
2
Define the key term 'bisharah'
Understand that 'bisharah' linguistically means news that changes one's complexion (basharah) from joy, and customarily it must be joyful and novel.
3
Apply to separate delivery
If slaves come one after another, only the first achieves the full effect of bisharah because subsequent reports lose the element of surprise and joyful change. Thus, condition is fulfilled only for the first.
4
Apply to simultaneous delivery
If all come together, each delivers the news at the same moment, so the condition is simultaneously fulfilled for all. Hence all are freed.
5
Summarize the legal maxim
The ruling hinges on the nature of bisharah: it is a singular event that cannot be repeated, but can be shared if occurring at the same time.

باب اليمين في العتق والطلاق. اشرف الہدایہ شرح اردو ہدایہ جلد ششم آقا نے کہا کہ ہر وہ غلام جو مجھے فلاں بیوی کے ہاں ولادت کی خوشخبری دے وہ آزاد ہے، تین غلاموں نے اسے علیحدہ علیحدہ خوشخبری دی، پہلے والا آزاد ہو جائیگا وَمَنْ قَالَ كُلُّ عَبْدِ بَشَّرَنِي بِوِلَا دَةِ فُلَانَةٍ فَهُوَ حُرِّ فَبَشَّرَهُ ثَلَثَةٌ مُتَفَرِّقِينَ عَتَقَ الْاَوَّلُ لِاَنَّ الْبَشَارَةَ اِسْمٌ لِخَيْرِ يُغَيِّرُ بَشْرَةَ الْوَجْهِ وَيَشْتَرِطُ كَوْنُهُ سَارًا بِالْعُرْفِ وَهَذَا إِنَّمَا يَتَحَقَّقُ مِنَ الْأَوَّلِ وَإِنْ بَشَّرُوهُ مَعَاعَتَقُوا لِأَنَّهَا تَحَقَّقَتْ مِنَ الْكُلِّ ترجمہ اور اگر کسی نے یہ کہا کہ ہر وہ غلام جو مجھے میری فلاں بیوی کے ہاں ولادت کی خوشخبری دے گا وہ آزاد ہوگا۔ پھر تین غلاموں نے اسے علیحدہ علیحدہ خوش خبری سنائی یعنی ایک کے بعد ایک نے تو ان میں سے پہلے جس نے بشارت سنائی وہ آزاد ہو جائے گا کیونکہ بشارت ایسی خبر کو کہتے ہیں جو چہرہ کی رنگت بدل دے لیکن عرف میں اس میں یہ بھی شرط ہے کہ وہ کوئی خوش خبری ہو۔ یعنی خوشی کی وجہ سے اس کے چہرہ کا رنگ متغیر ہو جائے اور یہ بات صرف پہلے غلام کی خبر سے ہی مکمل ہو جائے گی اور اگر ان تینوں نے مل کر ایک ساتھ اسے خوش خبری سنائی تو تینوں ہی آزاد ہو جائیں گے کیونکہ وہ بشارت ان تینوں سے پائی گئی ہے
Chapter of Oath regarding Manumission and Divorce. Ashraf al-Hidayah commentary on Hidayah, volume six. The master said: Every slave who gives me the good news of the birth of a certain wife is free. Three slaves gave him good news separately; the first will be free. And whoever said: Every slave who gives me the good news of the birth of so-and-so is free, then three gave him good news separately, the first is freed because 'bisharah' is a name for news that changes the complexion of the face, and by custom it is required to be joyful, and this is only realized from the first. And if they give him good news together, they are all freed because it was realized from all of them. Translation: And if someone said: Every slave who gives me the good news of the birth of my wife so-and-so will be free. Then three slaves gave him good news separately, i.e., one after the other, then the first who gave the good news will be free because 'bisharah' refers to news that changes the color of the face, and by custom it is also required that it be a joyful news, i.e., his facial color changes due to joy. This is only fully realized from the first slave's news. And if the three gave him good news together, then all three will be free because the good news was realized from all of them.
Source pages p. 107

Commentary
This passage discusses a conditional oath regarding manumission. The ruling states that when a man says, 'Every slave who gives me news of the birth of my wife is free,' and multiple slaves bring the news, the first to bring it is freed if they come separately. The reasoning is that the term 'bisharah' (good news) linguistically refers to news that changes one's complexion (from basharah, skin), and custom requires it to be joyful. This effect is only achieved with the first person who delivers the news, as the hearer's surprise and joy diminish with subsequent reports. However, if all slaves deliver the news simultaneously, they are all freed because the good news is realized from each of them, as the condition is fulfilled simultaneously. This ruling is based on linguistic and customary understanding, not on a primary textual proof.

Key Terms
بشارة (bishārah): News that changes the complexion of the face due to joy; good news that is joyful by custom.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a man says, 'Every slave who gives me news of the birth of my wife is free,' and two slaves give him the news one after the other, who is freed?
A) Both slaves are freed.
B) Only the first slave is freed.
C) Neither is freed.
D) The last slave is freed.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi ruling on conditional manumission hinges on the nature of 'bisharah' as a singular joyful event, freeing only the first deliverer if separate, but all if simultaneous.

Buying One's Father for Expiation: Cause or Condition?
Dispute on buying father for expiation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school considers buying one's father as valid for oath expiation and identify the disputed cause ('illah) of emancipation.

Core Idea
The purchase of a close relative is itself the cause of emancipation, not mere kinship.

The Hanafi school holds a unique position on using the purchase of a close relative to fulfill an expiation oath. While other schools see a contradiction between purchasing and emancipating, the Hanafis rely on a prophetic hadith and Arabic linguistic analogy to treat the purchase as the very act of emancipation. This lesson unpacks the inter-madhhab dispute and the underlying legal reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two rulings in the passage
First, a general case: buying a slave to fulfill an expiation after a conditional emancipation oath. Second, the specific case: buying one's own father for the same purpose. The first ruling is universally invalid; the second is disputed.
The first ruling sets the stage by showing that intention must be linked to the cause (oath), not the condition (purchase).
2
State the Hanafi position on buying the father
The Hanafis rule it valid. The purchase itself is the cause ('illah) of emancipation when the slave is a close relative. The intention for expiation is thus correctly linked to the purchase.
This is the mufta bihi position from Zahir al-Riwayah.
3
State the opposing position (Zufar and al-Shafi'i)
They rule it invalid. For them, the cause is kinship (qarabah), not the purchase. The purchase is merely a condition (shart). Because the intention is attached to the purchase (condition) rather than the cause, the expiation fails.
They argue that purchase establishes ownership while emancipation removes it – a contradiction.
4
Analyse the Hanafi proof (dalil)
The Prophet's hadith: 'A child cannot repay his father unless he finds him a slave, buys him, and frees him.' The conjunction 'fa' indicates immediate sequence, equating purchase with emancipation. The analogy 'saqahu fa-rawahu' (he gave him water and quenched him) supports that an action can be both cause and effect simultaneously.
The hadith is ahad (single-chain) with zanni thubut and dalala, but it is authoritative enough to override the logical objection.
5
Identify the practical difference (thamarah al-khilaf)
If a Hanafi buys his father for expiation, it is valid. According to Zufar and al-Shafi'i, he must free another slave instead. This affects real-world fatwa-giving.
This is the key takeaway for a mufti.

مفہوم وخلاصہ یہ ہے جب اعتناق کا عطف شراء" - حرف ناء کے ساتھ ہو تو وہ کلام عرب میں تراخی بالزمان کا مقتضی ہوتا ہے۔ جب ایک فعل کا عطف دوسرے فعل پر حرف فاء کے ساتھ ہوتو دوسر افعل پہلے فعل کے ساتھ ثابت ہوتا ہے۔ جیسا کہ کلام عرب میں اس کی مثال ہے۔ ضـربـه فـاوجـعه، اطعمه فاشیعه و سقاه فارواہ مذکورہ نظیر میں سقا فعل ہے اور اردو ابھی فعل ہے، ان دونوں ( فعلوں) کا عطف حرف ف کیسا تھ ہے۔ جس کا معنی ہے اس کو پانی پلا کر سیراب کر دیا۔ کلام عرب کی اس نظیر ( مثال) سے مذکورہ مسئلہ کی مطابقت (جو کہ ہماری یعنی احناف وغیرہ کی تائید کرتی ہے ) یوں ہوگی کہ جس طرح پانی پلانے کے فعل سے (پینے والے کے ) سیراب ہونے کا فعل ثابت ہوتا ہے اسی طرح قرابت کے خریدنے پر منی فعل سے عشق کا فعل ثابت ہوتا ہے۔ واللہ اعلم بالصواب۔
If someone said: If I buy so-and-so, he is free, then he bought him intending it for his oath expiation, it is not valid. And if he said: If I buy so-and-so, he is free, then he bought him intending for expiation of his oath, it does not suffice because the condition is the conjunction of intention with the cause of emancipation, which is the oath. As for buying, it is its condition. And if he bought his father intending for expiation of his oath, it suffices according to us, contrary to Zufar and al-Shafi'i. Their argument: buying is the condition of emancipation, but the cause is kinship. This is because buying establishes ownership, and emancipation removes it, and there is contradiction between them. Our argument: buying a close relative is emancipation because of his saying ﷺ: A child cannot repay his father unless he finds him a slave and buys him and frees him. He made the purchase itself emancipation because nothing else is required. So it becomes analogous to the saying 'saqahu fa-rawahu'. Translation: And if he said: If I buy so-and-so, he is free. Then he bought that slave intending for his oath expiation, the expiation is not valid. Because the condition for validity is that the intention be joined to the cause of emancipation, which is the oath, while purchase is only the condition. That is, as soon as he buys, he becomes free. Expiation is only valid when the intention is connected to the cause of emancipation (the oath), which is not present here. Rather, it is connected to the condition (purchase). So expiation is not valid. If someone bought his father with the intention of fulfilling his oath expiation, it is valid according to us, contrary to Imam Zufar and al-Shafi'i. Their evidence: purchase is only the condition of emancipation, and the cause is kinship, because purchase establishes ownership and emancipation removes it, and there is contradiction. Our evidence: buying a close relative itself constitutes emancipation because the Prophet ﷺ said: A child cannot repay his father unless he finds him a slave and buys him and frees him. He made purchase itself emancipation because no other condition is required. This is analogous to the Arab saying 'saqahu fa-rawahu' (he gave him water and quenched him). Explanation: The case is that if a person said about a specific person: If I buy him, he is free, then he bought him with the intention of expiation from an oath, the expiation is not valid. Because the cause of emancipation is connected to the oath; when the condition (purchase) occurs, the emancipation happens immediately. For expiation to be valid, the intention must be connected to the cause, not the condition. In this case, he intended expiation at the time of purchase (condition), not at the time of the oath (cause). So expiation is not valid. But if he bought his father intending expiation, according to the Hanafis it is valid because the purchase itself is considered emancipation for a close relative. The dispute reduces to: all agree that intention must be connected to the cause. Zufar and al-Shafi'i consider kinship as the cause, so the intention is not connected to the cause (since he intended expiation at purchase). The Hanafis consider the purchase (ownership) as the cause, so the intention is connected to the cause. The Hanafi view is supported by the hadith (narrated by Muslim and the four Sunan) and the Arabic analogy 'saqahu fa-rawahu'.
Source pages p. 107

Commentary
This passage discusses the effect of intention in conditional manumission oaths. The first ruling: if a man says, 'If I buy so-and-so, he is free,' and then buys him with the intention that this emancipation serves as his expiation for an oath, the expiation is not valid. The reason is that the cause ('illah) of emancipation is the oath itself (the man's statement), not the purchase. The purchase is only the condition (shart) that triggers the effect. For expiation to be valid, the intention must be linked to the cause. Here, the intention is linked to the condition (purchase), not to the cause (oath), so the expiation fails. The second ruling: if he buys his own father with the intention of expiation, the Hanafis (Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, Muhammad) rule it valid, while Imam Zufar and al-Shafi'i rule it invalid. The disagreement hinges on what constitutes the cause of emancipation. The opponents argue that the cause is kinship (qarabah), not the purchase, because purchase establishes ownership while emancipation removes it; they are contradictory. The Hanafis argue that the purchase itself is the cause, based on the hadith of the Prophet ﷺ: 'A child cannot repay his father unless he finds him a slave, buys him, and frees him.' This hadith treats the purchase as the act of emancipation itself. The Arabic analogy 'saqahu fa-rawahu' (he gave him water and quenched him) illustrates that when the verb of giving water is immediately followed by the result (quenching) with the particle 'fa', the second action is considered concurrent with the first. Similarly, buying a close relative is immediately considered emancipation. Therefore, in the Hanafi view, when someone buys his father intending expiation, his intention is correctly linked to the cause (the purchase-as-emancipation), so expiation is valid. This ruling is from the Zahir al-Riwayah and is the relied-upon position.

Key Terms
علة ('illah): The cause or effective reason that brings about a legal effect; in this context, the cause of emancipation is either the oath or the purchase/kinship.
شرط (shart): A condition that must be present for the effect to occur, but is not the cause; here, purchase is the condition for emancipation.
قرابة (qarabah): Kinship relationship; in the dispute, whether kinship is the 'illah of emancipation when buying a close relative.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the cause ('illah) of emancipation when a person buys his own father?
A) The oath of the buyer
B) The purchase itself
C) The kinship relation
D) The intention of expiation

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school rules that buying one's father for oath expiation is valid because the purchase itself, not kinship, is the cause of emancipation, as proven by the hadith and Arabic linguistic usage.

Buying Umm Walad for Kaffarah Does Not Suffice
Ruling that purchasing umm walad for kaffarah does not fulfill oath

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling on using an umm walad for kaffarah and explain why the pre-existing right to freedom (istilad) prevents the emancipation from fulfilling the kaffarah obligation.

Core Idea
Emancipation for kaffarah must be solely due to the oath; pre-existing freedom rights invalidate it.

In this passage, the Hanafi school addresses a nuanced issue in conditional oaths. A person swears to free a slave as kaffarah if he buys her. If the slave is his own umm walad, her freedom is already established by motherhood, so buying her does not fulfill the kaffarah. We'll explore the legal reasoning and the distinction between the umm walad and a regular slave.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Case
Read the passage carefully. A man makes a conditional statement to his umm walad: 'If I buy you, you are free as kaffarah for my oath.' Then he purchases her. The question is: does this fulfill his kaffarah obligation?
The condition is clearly stated: purchase triggers freedom.
2
Distinguish the Two Scenarios
The passage contrasts this with a scenario involving a regular slave (qinn). In that case, buying the slave for kaffarah does suffice. Note the difference: here the slave woman is an umm walad, meaning she has a child by her master and thus already has a right to freedom upon his death.
The key distinction is the status of the slave: umm walad vs. qinn.
3
Extract the Ruling for Umm Walad
The ruling is that she becomes free due to the condition ('fa-innaha tu'tiqu li-wujud al-shart'), but this does not count as kaffarah ('wa la yujzihi an al-kaffarah'). The emancipation is valid, but the kaffarah obligation remains.
The emancipation is a separate legal effect from the kaffarah.
4
Identify the Legal Cause (Illa)
The reason given is 'li-anna hurriyataha mustahaqqatun bil-istilad' (because her freedom is already due through motherhood). Her right to freedom pre-existed the oath, so the emancipation cannot be attributed solely to the oath for the purpose of kaffarah.
Istilad creates an independent entitlement to freedom.
5
Contrast with the Regular Slave
For a regular slave, there is no pre-existing right to freedom. Therefore, the emancipation is entirely attributable to the oath, and kaffarah is fulfilled. This highlights the principle that the emancipation must be solely a result of the oath to count.
The contrast clarifies the boundary of the rule.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب اليمين فى العتق والطلاق. وَ لَوِ اشْتَرَى أُمَّ وَلَدِهِ لَمْ يَجُزْ وَ مَعْنَى هَذَا الْمَسْأَلَةِ أَنْ يَقُولَ لَامَةٍ قَدِ اسْتَوْلَدَهَا بِالنِّكَاحِ إِنِ اشْتَرَيْتُكِ فَأَنْتِ حُرَّةٌ عَنْ كَفَّارَةِ يَمِيْنِى ثُمَّ اشْتَرَاهَا فَإِنَّهَا تَعْتِقُ لِوُجُودِ الشَّرْطِ وَلَا يُجْزِيْهِ عَنِ الْكَفَّارَةِ لِأَنَّ حُرِّيَّتَهَا مُسْتَحِقَّةٌ بِالاسْتِبْلَادِ فَلَا تُضَافُ إِلَى الْيَمِينِ مِنْ كُلِّ وَجْهِ بِخِلَافِ مَا إِذَا قَالَ لِقِنَّةٍ إِنِ اشْتَرَيْتُكِ فَأَنْتِ حُرَّةٌ عَنْ كَفَّارَةِ يَمِينِ حَيْثُ يُجْزِيْهِ عَنْهَا إِذَا اشْتَرَاهَا لَاتَ حُرِّيَّتَهَا غَيْرُ مُسْتَحِقَّةٌ بِجِهَةٍ أُخْرَى فَلَمْ يَخْتَلَّ الْإِضَافَةُ إِلَى الْيَمِينِ وَقَدْ قَارَنَتْهُ البَيِّه
Chapter of oaths regarding manumission and divorce. And if he buys his umm walad, it is not permissible. The meaning of this issue: he says to a slave woman whom he has made an umm walad through marriage: 'If I buy you, you are free for the kaffarah of my oath.' Then he buys her. She becomes free due to the existence of the condition, but it does not suffice for the kaffarah because her freedom is already due through istilad. So it is not attributed to the oath in every aspect. Contrary to if he said to a female slave [not umm walad]: 'If I buy you, you are free for the kaffarah of my oath' – then it suffices when he buys her because her freedom is not already due through another channel. So the attribution to the oath is not defective, and the intention has accompanied it.
Source pages p. 109

Commentary
This ruling from the chapter of oaths regarding manumission and divorce clarifies a nuance in conditional oaths. A person makes a conditional oath that if he purchases his umm walad (a slave woman who has borne him a child), she will be free as kaffarah for his oath. Upon purchase, the condition is met and she becomes free. However, this emancipation does not fulfill the kaffarah obligation because her freedom was already established by virtue of being an umm walad (istilad). The emancipation through the oath is secondary; the primary cause is the pre-existing right. In contrast, if the same statement is made about an unrelated slave woman (qinn), purchasing her fulfills the kaffarah because there is no pre-existing right to her freedom. This distinction is based on the principle that attributing emancipation to the oath is only valid when the emancipation is solely due to the oath.

Key Terms
أم ولد (umm walad): A slave woman who has borne a child to her master, thereby acquiring a special status that restricts her sale and guarantees her freedom upon her master's death.
كفارة (kaffarah): Expiation for a broken oath, typically freeing a slave, feeding, or clothing.
يمين (yamin): An oath, here a conditional oath suspending emancipation on a future event.
استيلاد (istilad): The act of a master fathering a child with his slave woman, which establishes her right to freedom upon his death.
قنة (qinn): A female slave without any special status or pre-existing right to freedom.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does purchasing one's umm walad for kaffarah not fulfill the oath?
A) Because the purchase was not accompanied by intention
B) Because her freedom was already due through istilad
C) Because she is not considered a slave for kaffarah
D) Because the condition was not met

Key Takeaway
A pre-existing right to freedom, such as that of an umm walad, prevents emancipation for kaffarah from fulfilling the obligation because the freedom must be solely attributable to the oath.

Oath to Free Slave Girl Upon Intercourse: Dispute with Zufar
Ruling that oath to free slave girl upon intercourse applies only to those owned at oath time; Zufar differs

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on whether an oath to free a slave girl upon intercourse applies to subsequently acquired slave girls, and explain the reasoning behind the dispute with Imam Zufar.

Core Idea
An oath to free a slave girl upon intercourse applies only to those owned at oath time – ownership is implied only for the condition, not the consequence.

This lesson examines a conditional oath: a man says, 'If I have intercourse with a slave girl, she is free.' The key legal question is whether this oath covers slave girls he later purchases. The Hanafi majority (Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn) hold that it does not, while Imam Zufar disagrees. Understanding this dispute clarifies how conditions in oaths are interpreted in Hanafi fiqh.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify the clear Hanafi ruling from the passage: if the condition is met with a slave girl owned at the time of the oath, she is freed; if the slave girl is acquired later, she is not freed.
This is the mufta bihi position.
2
Identify the opponent and their view
Note Imam Zufar's position: he holds that the oath applies to all slave girls, including those later acquired, because tasarri implies ownership.
Zufar is a Hanafi scholar, so this is an intra-madhhab dispute.
3
Follow the opponent's proof
Understand Zufar's reasoning: tasarri (intercourse with a concubine) is only valid with an owned slave girl, so mentioning tasarri implicitly includes ownership. Thus the condition covers all owned slave girls, present and future.
He draws an analogy with a divorce case where marriage is implied.
4
Analyse the Hanafi response
Examine the Hanafi rebuttal: ownership is implied only to the extent necessary for the validity of the condition (tasarri), not for the consequence (freedom). The condition is limited to its own scope.
The divorce analogy is used against Zufar: in that case, marriage is implied for the condition but not the consequence.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
Identify the practical difference: if a man buys a slave girl after the oath and has intercourse, the Hanafi majority says she is not freed; Zufar says she is freed.
This shows how a narrow interpretative principle yields different rulings.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَ مَنْ قَالَ إِنْ تَسَرَّيْتُ جَارِيَةً فَهِيَ حُرَّةٌ فَتَسَرَى جَارِيَةٌ كَانَتْ فِي مِلْكِهِ عَتَقَتْ لِأَنَّ الْيَمِيْنَ الْعَقَدَتْ فِي حَقِهَا لِمُصَادَ فَتِهَا الْمِلْكَ وَهَذَالاَنَّ الْجَارِيَّةَ مُنْكِرَةٌ فِي هَذَا الشَّرْطِ فَيَتَنَا وَلُ كُلَّ جَارِيَةٍ عَلَى الْإِنْفِرَادِ وَإِنِ اشْتَرَى جَارِيَةً فَتَسْرَاهَا لَمْ تَعْتِقْ بِهَذِهِ الْيَمِينِ خِلَافًا لِزُفَرَفَإِنَّهُ يَقُولُ التَّسَرِى لَا يَصِحُ إِلَّا فِي الْمِلْكِ فَكَانَ ذِكْرُهُ ذِكْرَ الْمِلْكِ فَصَارَ كَمَا إِذَا قَالَ لا جُنَبِيَّةِ إِنْ طَلَّقْتُكِ فَعَبْدِى حُرِّيصِيرُ التَّزَوُّجُ مَذْكُورًا وَلَنَا أَنَّ الْمِلْكَ يَصِيرُ مَذْكُورًا ضَرُورَةَ صِحَّةِ التَّسَرَى وَهُوَ شَرْطٌ فَيَتَقَدَّرُ بِقَدْرِهِ فَلَا يَظْهَرُ فِى حَقٍ صِحَّةِ الْجَزَاءِ وَهُوَ الْحُرِّيَّةُ وَفِي مَسْأَلَةِ الطَّلَاقِ إِنَّمَا يَظْهَرُ فِي حَقِ الشَّرْطِ دُونَ الْجَزَاءِ حَتَّى لَوْ قَالَ لَهَا إِنْ طَلَّقْتُكِ فَأَنْتِ طَالِقٌ ثَلَثًا فَتَزَوَّجَهَا وَطَلَّقَهَا لَا تُطَلِّقُ ثَلَكًا فَهَذِهِ وِزَانُ مَسْأَلَتِنَا
And whoever says: 'If I have intercourse with a slave girl, she is free,' then he has intercourse with a slave girl who is in his ownership – she becomes free because the oath applies to her since it finds her in his ownership. And this is because 'slave girl' is indefinite in this condition, so it encompasses every individual slave girl. If he buys a slave girl and then has intercourse with her, she does not become free by this oath, contrary to Zufar, because he says: 'Tasarri is only valid in ownership, so mentioning it is mentioning ownership. Thus it becomes like if he said to an unrelated woman, "If I divorce you, my slave is free," where marriage becomes mentioned.' And our argument is that ownership becomes mentioned out of necessity for the validity of tasarri, and it is a condition, so it is limited to its extent. Thus it does not appear in the validity of the consequence, which is freedom. And in the issue of divorce, it appears in the condition but not in the consequence. So if he said to her: 'If I divorce you, you are thrice divorced,' then marries her and divorces her, she is not thrice divorced. This is analogous to our issue.
Source pages p. 109

Commentary
This passage presents a Hanafi ruling on conditional oaths regarding slave girls, including a dispute with Imam Zufar. The scenario: A person says, 'If I have intercourse with a slave girl, she is free.' This is a suspensory oath. The ruling: If he has intercourse with a slave girl he owned at the time of the oath, she becomes free because the indefinite term 'jariyah' covers any slave girl in his possession. However, if he purchases a slave girl after the oath and then has intercourse with her, she is not freed by this oath. Imam Zufar disagreed, arguing that 'tasarri' (intercourse with a concubine) is only valid in ownership, so mentioning tasarri implies ownership as part of the condition. Thus the oath should also apply to later acquired slave girls. The Hanafi response: Ownership is implied only to the extent necessary for the validity of tasarri (the condition), not for the consequence (freedom). This is analogous to a divorce case: if one says to an unrelated woman, 'If I divorce you, my slave is free,' marriage is implied for the condition but not for the consequence. So if he later marries and divorces her, the slave is not freed. Similarly, here ownership is implied only for the condition, so the freedom only applies to those owned at the time of the oath.

Key Terms
تسري (tasarri): Taking a slave woman as a concubine, i.e., having sexual intercourse with one's female slave.
جار (jariyah): A female slave; in the oath it is indefinite implying generality.
شرط (shart): Condition in an oath; here the act of tasarri triggers the consequence.
جزاء (jaza): The consequence of the condition being fulfilled; here the emancipation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school (majority), if a person says 'If I have intercourse with a slave girl, she is free' and later buys a slave girl and has intercourse with her, what happens?
A) She becomes free because the oath covers any slave girl
B) She does not become free because ownership at time of oath is required
C) She becomes free only if the purchase was intended for kaffarah
D) She becomes free only if she is an umm walad

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, conditions in oaths are interpreted narrowly – ownership implied for the validity of the condition does not extend to the consequence of freedom.

Oath Not to Beat or Slaughter: Effect of Commanding Another
Ruling on commanding another after swearing not to do a physical act

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on whether commanding another to perform a physical act breaks an oath not to do it yourself, and explain the distinction from speech acts like divorce.

Core Idea
Commanding another to beat a slave or slaughter a sheep breaks your oath because the owner is considered the direct doer.

In this passage from al-Hidayah, we examine a ruling on oaths concerning physical acts. When you swear not to beat your slave or slaughter your sheep, and then command another to do it, the oath is broken. The key is that the owner has authority and the benefit returns to him, so he is treated as the direct doer. This contrasts with oaths about speech acts like divorce, where commanding another is treated differently. Understanding this distinction is crucial for applying Hanafi law on oaths.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the oath
Recognize that the oath is about a physical act (beating or slaughter) that the swearer has authority over.
2
General rule: commanding another breaks the oath
The default Hanafi ruling: if you command another to do the act after swearing not to, you break the oath.
3
Understand the proof
The owner has authority to delegate, and the benefit of the act returns to the commander, so he is considered the direct doer (mubāshir) via causation (tasbīb).
4
Note the exception for intention
If the swearer says 'I intended only not to do it personally,' he is believed both in court (qaḍā’an) and conscience (diyānatan).
5
Contrast with divorce (speech act)
For speech acts like divorce, commanding another is treated as speaking, so an intention to only speak personally is accepted only in conscience, not court.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَلَوْ حَلَفَ لَا يَضْرِبُ عَبْدَهُ اَوْلَا يَذْبَحُ شَاتَهُ فَأَمَرَغَيْرَهُ فَفَعَلَ يَحْنِتُ فِي يَمِينِهِ لِاَنَّ الْمَالِكَ لَهُ وَلَايَةُ ضَرْب عَبْدِهِ وَذَبْحِ شَاتِهِ فَيَمْلِكُ تَوْلِيَتَهُ غَيْرُهُ ثُمَّ مَنْفَعَتُهُ رَاجِعَةٌ إِلَى الْامِرِ فَيُجْعَلُ هُوَ مُبَاشِرًا إِذْلَا حُقُوقَ لَهُ يَرْجِعُ إِلَى الْمَامُورِو لَوْقَالَ عَلَيْتُ أَنْ لَّا اَوْلِيَ ذَلِكَ بِنَفْسِي دُيّنَ فِي الْقَضَاءِ بِخِلَافِ مَاتَقَدَّمَ مِنَ الطَّلَاقِ وَغَيْرِهِ وَوَجْهُ الْفَرْقِ أَنَّ الطَّلَاقَ لَيْسَ إِلَّا تَكَلُّمًا بِكَلَامٍ يُفْضِى إِلَى وُقُوعِ الطَّلَاقِ عَلَيْهَا وَالْأَمْرُ بِذَالِكَ مِثْلُ التَّكَلَّمِ بِهِ وَاللَّفْظُ يَنتَظِمُهُمَا فَإِذَانَوِي التَّكَلَّمَ بِهِ فَقَدْ نَوَى الْخُصُوص فِي الْعَامِ فَيُدَيَّنُ دِيَانَةٌ لَا قَضَاءً اَمَّا الذَّبْحُ وَالضَّرْبُ فِعْلٌ حَسَى يُعْرَفُ بِاثْرِهِ وَالنِّسْبَةُ إِلَى الْأمر بالتَّسْبِيبِ مَجَازًا فَإِذَا نَوَى الْفِعْلَ بِنَفْسِهِ فَقَدْ نَوَى الْحَقِيْقَةَ فَيُصَدِّقْ دِيَانَةٌ وَقَضَاءً
And if he swears, 'I will not beat his slave, nor slaughter his sheep,' and then commands another and he does it, he breaks his oath, because the owner has authority to beat his slave and slaughter his sheep, so he has the power to delegate that to another. Then its benefit returns to the commander, so he is made the direct doer, since there are no rights that return to the commanded. And if he says, 'I intended that I would not personally perform that,' he is believed in court, contrary to what preceded of divorce and others. And the face of the distinction: divorce is merely speaking words that lead to the occurrence of divorce on her, and commanding that is like speaking it, and the word comprehends both. So if he intends to speak it, he has intended the specific within the general, so he is believed in religion but not in court. As for slaughter and beating, it is a physical act known by its effect, and attributing it to the commander is metaphorical, due to causation. So if he intends the act by himself, he has intended the literal, so he is believed both in religion and in court.
Source pages p. 113

Commentary
This passage discusses a third category: physical acts of beating a slave or slaughtering a sheep. If someone swears not to do these acts and then commands another to do them, the oath is broken. Reason: the owner has full authority over these acts and can delegate them. The benefit returns to the commander, so he is considered the direct doer (mubāshir), because there are no separate rights belonging to the commanded person. If the swearer says he meant only not to do it personally, he is believed both in court (qaḍā’an) and in conscience (diyānatan). This differs from the earlier case of divorce, etc., where the intention to not speak was accepted only in conscience. The distinction: divorce is speech; commanding speech is like speaking, so the oath covers both. But beating/slaughter is a physical act; attributing it to the commander by causation (tasbīb) is metaphorical, so his intention to exclude personal action is literal and accepted in court.

Key Terms
تسبيب (tasbīb): Causation; attributing an act to someone who commanded or caused it, even if not the direct doer.
مباشر (mubāshir): The direct doer of an act.
ديانة وقضاء (diyānatan wa qaḍā’an): In religious conscience and in court; both realms of validity.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If someone swears 'I will not slaughter my sheep' and then commands his servant to slaughter it, what is the Hanafi ruling?
A) The oath is not broken because the servant did it.
B) The oath is broken because the owner is considered the direct doer.
C) The oath is broken only if the owner intended to include the servant.
D) The oath is not broken because slaughter is a physical act.

Key Takeaway
The oath not to perform a physical act is broken when you command another, unless you intended only personal action—contrary to speech acts where intention is only accepted in conscience.

Oath Not to Marry/Divorce/Free: Effect of Wakeel and Intention
Ruling on oaths of personal status via wakeel

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that appointing a wakeel for personal status acts breaks the oath, and distinguish between diyanah and qada' acceptance of intention.

Core Idea
In personal status acts, a wakeel is only a messenger; his act is the principal's act, breaking the oath.

This lesson examines a key Hanafi rule: when a person swears not to perform a personal status act (marriage, divorce, freeing) and then appoints a wakeel to do it, the oath is still broken. The reasoning hinges on the nature of the wakeel as a safir (messenger) – his speech is attributed to the principal. We also explore the diyanah/qada’ distinction when the swearer claims a limited intention.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario and ruling
The swearer vows not to marry, divorce, or free a slave. He then appoints a wakeel who performs the act. The ruling is that the swearer becomes hanith (oath-breaker).
This holds even though the swearer did not personally utter the words.
2
Understand the proof: wakeel as safir
The reason is that in such matters, the wakeel is merely a messenger (safir) who conveys the principal's speech. He attributes the act to the principal, not to himself.
Example: wakeel says 'My principal married you', not 'I married you'.
3
Apply the diyanah/qada' distinction
If the swearer claims he intended only that he himself would not speak (not that he would avoid delegation), his claim is believed diyanatan (in conscience) but not qada'an (in court).
The qadi judges by the apparent general wording of the oath.
4
Note the forthcoming distinction with sale/lease
The text promises a future explanation of why sale and lease differ from marriage/divorce/freeing in this context. For now, understand that the wakeel's role is different in commercial contracts.
In sale, the wakeel acts with more independent authority.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب اليمين في البيع والشراء والتزوج وغير ذالك .......... . اشرف الہدایہ شرح اردو ہدایہ جلد ششم ترجمہ اگر کسی نے یہ قسم کھائی کہ میں نکاح نہیں کروں گا یا طلاق نہیں دوں گا یا آزاد نہیں کروں گا پھر اس نے ان کاموں کے لئے کسی کو وکیل بنا دیا اور اس نے کام پورے کر دیئے تو حانث ہو جائے گا۔ کیونکہ ایسے معاملات میں کام کرنے والا وکیل صرف ایک سفیر کی حیثیت سے ہوتا ہے۔ یعنی اس نے دوسرے کی بات بیان کر دی ہے۔ اس لئے وہ نکاح یا طلاق یا حق کے معاملات کو اپنی طرف منسوب کر کے یہ نہیں کہتا ہے کہ مثلاً میں نے تم سے نکاح کیا۔ یا میں نے تم کو طلاق دی۔ بلکہ اپنے مؤکل کی طرف منسوب کر کے کہتا ہے کہ مثلاً میرے موکل نے تمہارے ساتھ نکاح کیا یا آزاد کیا یا طلاق دی۔ اور یہ معاملات ایسے ہوتے ہیں کہ ان کے حقوق سارے کے سارے موکل ہی سے تعلق رکھتے ہیں اور وہی ان کا ذمہ دار ہوتا ہے۔ اور اگر ایسی قسمیں کھانے والے نے کہا کہ میری مراد صرف یہ تھی کہ میں نکاح یا طلاق یا عتناق کے کام میں خود نہیں بولوں گا یعنی اس نے یہ چاہا کہ وکیل بنانے سے حانث نہ ہو تو دیانت کے طور پر تو اس کی بات صحیح مانی جا سکتی ہے لیکن قاضی اس کی تصدیق نہیں کرے گا۔ اس کا فرق انشاء اللہ ہم عنقریب بیان کر دیں گے۔ فائدہ یعنی خرید و فروخت اور اجارہ میں اور نکاح و طلاق دو عتاق میں جو فرق ہے اس کی تفصیل آئندہ آئیگی۔
Chapter on oaths in sale, purchase, marriage, and other matters. Ashraf al-Hidaya Urdu commentary on Hidaya, volume 6. Translation: If someone swore that he will not marry, or not divorce, or not free a slave, then he appointed a wakeel for these actions and the wakeel completed them, he becomes hanith. Because in such matters, the acting wakeel is only in the capacity of a messenger. That is, he conveyed the words of another. Therefore, he does not attribute marriage, divorce, or freeing to himself by saying, for example, 'I married you' or 'I divorced you'; rather, he attributes to his principal by saying, for example, 'My principal married you' or freed or divorced. And these matters are such that all their rights pertain to the principal and he is responsible for them. And if the swearer said, 'My intention was only that I myself would not speak in the matter of marriage, divorce, or freeing' – meaning he intended that appointing a wakeel would not make him hanith – then as a matter of conscience his statement can be accepted as true, but the qadi will not confirm it. We will soon explain the difference, God willing. Benefit: The distinction between sale and lease on one hand and marriage, divorce, and freeing on the other will be explained later.
Source pages p. 113

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with reasoning from the sharh. It states that if a person swears not to marry, divorce, or free a slave, and then appoints a wakeel to do it, the swearer becomes hanith (liable for breaking the oath). The reason is that the wakeel in such personal status matters is merely a safir (messenger) who speaks on behalf of the principal; the rights and consequences belong to the principal. If the swearer claims he only meant that he himself would not utter the words, he is believed diyanatan (according to his conscience) but not qada'an (in court), because the general wording includes both direct and indirect speech. A future explanation is promised for the distinction between sale/lease and marriage/divorce/freeing.

Key Terms
وکیل (wakīl): A representative appointed to perform an act, but in personal status acts he is a messenger, not a substitute.
سفیر (safīr): A messenger who simply conveys the words of another, without personal legal capacity in the act.
حانث (hānith): One who breaks an oath, thereby becoming liable for expiation or consequences.
دیانت (diyānatan): In conscience; the person's own claim is accepted as true between him and Allah, but not enforced by the qadi.
قضاء (qaḍā'an): In court; the judge does not accept the person's claim against apparent evidence.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If someone swears not to divorce his wife, then appoints a wakeel who pronounces the divorce, what is the ruling?
A) He does not become hanith because he did not personally utter the divorce.
B) He becomes hanith because the wakeel is a messenger and the act is attributed to him.
C) He becomes hanith only if he intended to divorce through the wakeel.
D) He is not responsible because the wakeel acted on his own authority.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, a wakeel in marriage, divorce, or freeing is a safir; thus, if you swear not to do these acts, the wakeel's action breaks your oath.

Oath on Beating or Slaughtering: Ordering Another and the Distinction from Speech Acts
Ruling on ordering another for physical acts in oaths

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on when ordering another to perform a physical act breaks an oath and distinguish this from speech acts regarding acceptance of intention in court.

Core Idea
Ordering another to beat your slave breaks your oath because you are the cause and beneficiary; but if you intended only self-action, you are believed in court for physical acts.

This passage from al-Hidayah clarifies a subtle point in oath liability. When someone swears not to perform a physical act, like beating his slave or slaughtering his goat, and then orders another to do it, is he guilty of breaking the oath? The answer is yes, because he is the cause. But what if he claims he intended only not to do it himself? The Hanafi school makes a crucial distinction between physical acts and speech acts—a distinction with practical consequences in court.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the oath
The oath is about a physical act (beating or slaughtering). Determine what act is prohibited by the oath: the swearer's own direct action or any action by others?
Physical acts are known by their effect; speech acts are known by utterance.
2
Ordering another and its effect
If the swearer orders someone else to do the act and that person does it, does the swearer become hanith (break the oath)? According to the Hanafi school, yes, because the owner has authority over his property, and the benefit of the act returns to him. He is considered the direct doer via causation (tasbīb).
The deputy has no independent rights in the act; thus the act is attributed to the orderer.
3
Claiming intention of self-action
If the swearer claims that at the time of swearing he intended only not to do the act himself, is he believed? For physical acts, the answer is yes, both diyanatan (conscience) and qada'an (court). For speech acts like divorce, freeing, or marriage, the claim is accepted only diyanatan, not qada'an.
The basis of the distinction: divorce is pure speech; ordering it is like speaking it. The wording encompasses both. Physical acts are attributed metaphorically via causation.
4
Understand the basis of distinction
The key difference is that physical acts are 'maḥsūs fiʿl'—perceptible by senses. Intending self-action is the literal (ḥaqīqah) meaning of the oath. For speech acts, ordering is also a form of saying, so the literal meaning would have to be specified.
Thus, for physical acts, the swearer's claim is taken as literal, so he is believed in court.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَلَوْ حَلَفَ لَا يَضْرِبُ عَبْدَهُ اَوْلَا يَذْبَحُ شَاتَهُ فَأَمَرَغَيْرَهُ فَفَعَلَ يَحْنِتُ فِي يَمِينِهِ لِاَنَّ الْمَالِكَ لَهُ وَلَايَةُ ضَرْب عَبْدِهِ وَذَبْحِ شَاتِهِ فَيَمْلِكُ تَوْلِيَتَهُ غَيْرُهُ ثُمَّ مَنْفَعَتُهُ رَاجِعَةٌ إِلَى الْامِرِ فَيُجْعَلُ هُوَ مُبَاشِرًا إِذْلَا حُقُوقَ لَهُ يَرْجِعُ إِلَى الْمَامُورِو لَوْقَالَ عَلَيْتُ أَنْ لَّا اَوْلِيَ ذَلِكَ بِنَفْسِي دُيّنَ فِي الْقَضَاءِ بِخِلَافِ مَاتَقَدَّمَ مِنَ الطَّلَاقِ وَغَيْرِهِ وَوَجْهُ الْفَرْقِ أَنَّ الطَّلَاقَ لَيْسَ إِلَّا تَكَلُّمًا بِكَلَامٍ يُفْضِى إِلَى وُقُوعِ الطَّلَاقِ عَلَيْهَا وَالْأَمْرُ بِذَالِكَ مِثْلُ التَّكَلَّمِ بِهِ وَاللَّفْظُ يَنتَظِمُهُمَا فَإِذَانَوِي التَّكَلَّمَ بِهِ فَقَدْ نَوَى الْخُصُوص فِي الْعَامِ فَيُدَيَّنُ دِيَانَةٌ لَا قَضَاءً اَمَّا الذَّبْحُ وَالضَّرْبُ فِعْلٌ حَسَى يُعْرَفُ بِاثْرِهِ وَالنِّسْبَةُ إِلَى الْأمر بالتَّسْبِيبِ مَجَازًا فَإِذَا نَوَى الْفِعْلَ بِنَفْسِهِ فَقَدْ نَوَى الْحَقِيْقَةَ فَيُصَدِّقْ دِيَانَةٌ وَقَضَاءً ترجمہ اور اگر کسی نے یہ قسم کھائی کہ میں اپنے غلام کو نہیں ماروں گا یا اپنی بکری ذبح نہیں کروں گا۔ پھر اس نے کسی دوسرے کو حکم دیا اور اس نے یہ کام کر لئے۔ تو یہ اپنی قسم میں حانث ہو جائے گا۔ کیونکہ مالک کو اپنے غلام کے مارنے یا اپنی بکری کے بعد میں ایسے حقوق نہیں ہوتے ہیں جو نائب کی طرف منسوب ہوں۔ اور اگر قسم کھانے والے نے کہا کہ قسم کھاتے وقت ہی میری نیت یہ تھی کہ اس کام کو میں خود نہیں کروں گا۔ تو قاضی کے پاس کبھی اس کی بات کی تصدیق کی جائے گی۔ بخلاف مذکورہ مسائل طلاق وغیرہ کے یعنی طلاق و عتاق و نکاح میں قاضی ان کے قول کی تصدیق نہیں کرتا تھا۔ ان میں فرق کی وجہ یہ ہے کہ طلاق تو صرف کلام کرنے کا نام ہے جس سے بولتے ہی بیوی پر طلاق واقع ہو جائے۔ اور اس کام کا حکم دینا خود ہی کہنے کے مثل ہے۔ اور جس لفظ سے قسم کھائی ہے وہ خود بولنے اور دوسرے کی زبان سے کہلانے دونوں کو شامل ہے۔ پھر اگر اس نے خود ہی اس لفظ کے بولنے کی نیت کی تو اس نے عام میں خاص کی نیت کی اس بناء پر دیانتا تو اس کی تصدیق کی جائے گی۔ لیکن قاضی اس کی تصدیق نہیں کرے گا۔ لیکن مارنا اور ذبح کرنا تو ایک محسوس فعل ہے جو اپنی اصل سے پہچانا جاتا ہے۔ اور موکل کے طرف نسبت کرنا اس وجہ سے مجاز ہے کہ وہی سبب واقع ہوا ہے۔ پس اگر قسم کھانے والے نے بذات خود نہ کرنے کی نیت کی ہو تو اس نے اپنے کلام کے حقیقی معنی مراد لئے۔ اس لئے دیا تھا اس کی تصدیق ہوگی اور قاضی بھی اس کی تصدیق کرے گا۔
And if he swears not to beat his slave, or not to slaughter his sheep, then he orders someone else and that person does it, he becomes hanith in his oath. Because the owner has authority to beat his slave and slaughter his sheep, so he can delegate it to another. Then the benefit [of the act] returns to the orderer, so he is considered the direct doer, since there are no rights that revert to the one ordered. And if he said, 'I intended that I myself would not do it,' he is believed in court [qada'an], contrary to what was mentioned regarding divorce and other matters. The basis of the distinction is that divorce is only speech that leads to divorce occurring upon her, and ordering it is like speaking it; the wording encompasses both. So if he intends to speak it himself, he intended the specific within the general, so he is believed diyanatan, not qada'an. As for slaughter and beating, they are physical acts known by their effect, and attribution to the orderer is metaphorical [majaz] because he is the cause. So if he intends the act by himself, he intended the real meaning, so he is believed both diyanatan and qada'an. Translation: And if someone swore that he would not beat his slave or slaughter his goat, then he ordered another and that person did it, then he becomes hanith in his oath. Because the owner has the authority to beat his slave or slaughter his goat, so he can appoint another as his deputy. Then the benefit of the act goes to the owner, so he is considered the doer of those actions, because after such actions there are no rights that are attributed to the deputy. And if the swearer said, 'At the time of swearing my intention was that I myself would not do this act,' then the qadi will confirm his statement. Contrary to the aforementioned issues of divorce, etc., i.e., in divorce, freeing, and marriage, the qadi did not confirm their statement. The reason for the difference is that divorce is only speech that, when uttered, results in divorce. And ordering that act is like speaking it oneself. And the word used in the oath includes both speaking oneself and having it said by another. So if he intended to utter the word himself, he intended the specific within the general; on that basis, he is believed diyanatan, but the qadi will not confirm it. But beating and slaughtering are physical acts known by their source. And attributing them to the principal is metaphorical because he is the cause. So if the swearer intended not to do it himself, he intended the real meaning of his words. Therefore, he is believed in conscience [diyanatan] and the qadi will also confirm him.
Source pages p. 113

Commentary
This passage presents a ruling with proof and a distinction from the previous case. It states that if a person swears not to beat his slave or not to slaughter his sheep, and then orders another to do it, the swearer becomes hanith. The reason is that the owner has full authority over his property, so when he delegates, the act is attributed to him because the benefit returns to him and the deputy has no rights in the act. However, if the swearer claims that he only intended not to do the act himself, he is believed both diyanatan and qada'an (in court). This contrasts with the earlier ruling on divorce and similar speech acts, where the claim is only accepted diyanatan. The distinction is that divorce is a speech act; ordering it is like saying it, so the general wording covers both. But beating and slaughtering are physical acts (hissi), and attribution to the orderer is metaphorical (through causation). Therefore, if the swearer intends the literal meaning (self-action), he is believed in court as well. This shows the Hanafi precision in distinguishing between speech acts and physical acts in oath liability.

Key Terms
محسوس فعل (maḥsūs fiʿl): A physical act perceived by the senses, as opposed to a speech act.
مجاز (majāz): Metaphorical usage; here, attribution of the act to the orderer is metaphorical because he is the cause.
تسبیب (tasbīb): Causation; the orderer is the cause of the act, hence attribution.
حقیقت (ḥaqīqah): Literal meaning; here, the swearer's intention to do the act himself is the literal interpretation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to this passage, why is the swearer believed in court (qada'an) when he says he intended not to beat his slave himself, but not for divorce?
A) Because divorce is more serious.
B) Because beating is a physical act, and intending self-action is the literal meaning of the oath.
C) Because the slave is property.
D) Because the qadi trusts the swearer more in beating cases.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, ordering another to do a physical act breaks an oath, but if the swearer proves he intended only self-action, he is believed in court, unlike for speech acts where the claim is only accepted in conscience.

Oath Not to Beat Child: Ordering Another Does Not Break Oath
Ruling that ordering another to beat one's child does not make one hanith

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the principle of benefit (manfa'ah) to determine whether an oath is broken when one orders another to perform the prohibited act.

Core Idea
Attribution of an act follows the benefit: if benefit goes to the actor, the orderer is not the doer.

A person swears not to beat his child, then orders someone else to do it. Does the oath break? The answer depends on who benefits from the act. This lesson unpacks the Hanafi criterion of benefit (manfa'ah) that determines legal attribution. Understanding this principle clarifies many similar oath-related cases.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core ruling
The passage states that if a person swears not to beat his child and orders another to beat the child, he does NOT become hanith (oath-breaker).
2
Extract the legal cause (ʿillah)
The justification given is that the benefit of beating the child (discipline, reform) returns to the child himself, not to the orderer.
This is the key distinction: benefit (manfa'ah) determines to whom the act is attributed.
3
Contrast with the slave case
If the order is to beat a slave, the benefit (obedience and fear) returns to the master, so the act is attributed to the orderer, making him hanith.
The slave case is given as a counterexample to highlight the rule.
4
Apply the principle to other scenarios
The principle of benefit attribution can be extended: any act where the primary benefit goes to the performer (or a third party) will not be attributed to the orderer for oath purposes.
This is a talīl (reasoning) that can be used for analogous cases.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَمَنْ حَلَفَ لَا يَضْرِبْ وَلَدَه فَاَمَرَ انْسَانًا فَضَرَبَةِ لَمْ يَحْنِتُ فِي يَمِينِهِ لِأَنَّ مَنْفَعَةَ ضَرْبِ الْوَلَدِ عَائِدَةٌ إِلَيْهِ وَهُوَ الصَّابُ وَالتَّشَقَّفُ فَلَمْ يُنسَبْ فِعْلَهُ إِلَى الْامِرِ بِخِلَافِ الْأَمْرِ بِضَرْبِ الْعَبْدِلَانَّ مَنْفَعَتَهُ الْإِيْتِمَارُ بِأَمْرِهِ فَيُضَافُ الْفِعْلُ إِلَيْهِ ترجمہ اور اگر کسی نے یہ قسم کھائی کہ میں اپنے لڑکے کو نہیں ماروں گا۔ پھر اس نے اپنے کسی آدمی کو حکم دیا اور اس نے اسے مارا تو یہ اپنی قسم میں حانث نہیں ہوگا۔ کیونکہ بچہ کے مارنے کا نتیجہ اور نفع خود بچہ کو پہنچتا ہے۔ یعنی وہ ادب سیکھتا ہے۔ اور راہ راست پر آ جاتا ہے۔ اس لئے اس کے نائب کا فعل اس کے حکم دینے والے کی طرف منسوب نہ ہو گا۔ بخلاف اس کے اگر غلام کو مارنے کے لئے کسی کو حکم دیا تو اس نائب کا مارنا اس مسئلہ میں خود اس حکم دہندہ کے مارنے کے حکم میں ہوگا۔ کیونکہ اس مار کا فائدہ یہ ہوگا کہ وہ غلام اپنے مولیٰ کے حکم کے خلاف کرنے سے خائف ہوگا۔ اور اس کی فرماں برداری کی کوشش کرے گا۔ اس لئے مارنے کا فعل اس کے مولیٰ کی طرف منسوب ہوگا۔
And whoever swore not to beat his child, then ordered a person and he beat him, he does not become hanith in his oath. Because the benefit of beating the child returns to him (the child) – namely, discipline and learning – so his act is not attributed to the orderer. Unlike the case of ordering to beat a slave, because the benefit of that is the slave's obedience to the master's command, so the act is attributed to him. Translation: And if someone swore that he would not beat his son, then he ordered a person and that person beat him, then he does not become hanith in his oath. Because the result and benefit of beating the child goes to the child himself – that is, he learns discipline and comes to the straight path. Therefore, the act of his deputy is not attributed to the orderer. Contrary to the case of ordering to beat a slave, because the benefit of that beating is that the slave will fear going against his master's order and try to obey him. Therefore, the act of beating is attributed to the master.
Source pages p. 114

Commentary
This passage gives a ruling with proof on beating a child. It distinguishes between beating a child and beating a slave. If a person swears not to beat his child, and then orders another to beat the child, he does not break his oath. The reason is that the benefit of disciplining a child goes to the child himself (education and reform), not to the parent. Therefore, the act cannot be attributed to the parent. In contrast, beating a slave benefits the master (by instilling obedience), so ordering another is attributed to the master. This demonstrates the Hanafi principle that legal attribution of an act follows the benefit (manfa'ah) of the act.

Key Terms
منفعة (manfaʿah): Benefit; in this context, the benefit of the act determines to whom it is attributed.
نائب (nāʾib): Deputy; the person who performs the act on behalf of another.
ولده (waladuh): His child; here specifically a son or child under guardianship.
عبد (ʿabd): Slave; considered property, so benefit of beating goes to the master.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does ordering another to beat one's child not break the oath, but ordering to beat a slave does?
A) Because a child has more rights.
B) Because the benefit of beating the child goes to the child (discipline), while beating a slave benefits the master (obedience).
C) Because beating a child is always forbidden.
D) Because the slave is property and the child is not.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, the attribution of an act ordered by another depends on who receives the primary benefit; if the benefit is not the orderer's, he does not become hanith.

The Particle 'Lam' in Oaths: Agency vs Ownership
Linguistic ruling on particle 'lam' in oaths

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between two oath formulations using the particle 'lam' and state when the oath is broken under Hanafi fiqh.

Core Idea
The placement of 'lam' determines if the oath requires the addressee's order or ownership.

This lesson explores a subtle grammatical distinction in oath formulations. The particle 'lam' can indicate either agency ('for you' meaning by your order) or ownership ('your cloth'). The ruling changes based on which word 'lam' is attached to, reflecting the Hanafi method of extracting legal rules from linguistic precision.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two formulations
First formulation: 'If I sell this cloth for you (li-ka)' – lam attached to the verb. Second: 'If I sell your cloth (thawban laka)' – lam attached to the noun.
The Arabic phrase differs: 'bi'tu laka' vs 'thawban laka'.
2
Note the position of lam
In the first, lam is on the verb; in the second, lam is on the noun (closer to it). This grammatical position is decisive.
3
Understand the effect
Lam on the verb implies agency: the sale must be by the addressee's order. Lam on the noun implies ownership: the cloth must belong to the addressee.
Because sale admits proxy (niyabah), the agency interpretation is plausible.
4
Apply the analogy
Acts like goldsmithing and tailoring, which admit proxy, follow the same rule. Acts like eating, drinking, beating a slave do not admit proxy, so the ruling does not differ between the two formulations.
This shows the scope of the principle.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَمَنْ قَالَ لِغَيْرِهِ إِنْ بِعْتُ لَكَ هَذَا التَّوْبَ فَامْرَأَتُهُ طَالِقٌ فَلَبَّسَ الْمَحْلُوفُ عَلَيْهِ ثَوْبَا فِي ثِيَابِ الْحَالِفِ فَبَاعَهُ وَلَمْ يَعْلَمُ لَمْ يَحْدِثُ لِاَنَّ حَرْفَ اللَّامِ دَخَلَ عَلَى الْبَيْعِ فَيَقْتَضِى اِخْتِصَاصَهُ بِهِ وَذَالِكَ بِأَنْ يَفْعَلَهُ بِأَمْرِهِ إِذِ الْبَيْعُ يَجْرِى فِيهِ النِّيَابَةُ وَلَمْ يُوجَدُ بِخِلَافِ مَا إِذَاقَالَ إِنْ بِعْتُ ثَوْبًا لَكَ حَيْثُ يَحْنِتُ إِذَابَاعَ ثَوْبًا مَمْلُو كَا لَهُ سَوَاءٌ كَانَ بِأَمْرِهِ أَوْ بِغَيْرِ اَمْرِهِ عَلِمَ بِذَالِكَ أَوَلَمْ يَعْلَمْ لِاَنَّ حَرْفَ اللَّامِ دَخَلَ عَلَى الْعَيْنِ لِأَنَّهُ أَقْرَبُ إِلَيْهِ فَيَقْتَضِى اخْتِصَاصَ الْعَيْنَ بِهِ وَذَالِكَ بِأَنْ يَكُونَ مَمْلُوكًا لَهُ وَنَظِيرُهُ الصَّيَاغَةُ وَالْخِيَاطَةُ وَكُلُّ مَا يَجْرِى فِيهِ النِّيَابَةُ بِخِلَافِ الأَكْلِ وَالشَّرْبِ وَصَرْبِ الْغُلَامِ لِأَنَّهُ لَا يَحْتَمِلُ النِّيَابَةَ فَلَا يَفْتَرِقُ الْحُكْمُ فِيْهِ فِي الْوَجْهَيْنِ ترجمہ اگر زید نے خالد سے کہا کہ اگر میں یہ کپڑا تمہارے لئے فروخت کروں تو میری بیوی کو طلاق ہے۔ اس کے بعد خالد نے اس کپڑے کو زید کے کپڑوں میں چھپا دیا۔ اور زید نے وہ سب کپڑے بیچ دیئے۔ اس وقت اسے یہ خبر نہیں تھی کہ وہ کپڑا بھی ان کپڑوں میں موجود ہے۔ اس لئے وہ حانث نہیں ہوا کیونکہ اس کا پہلے یہ کہنا کہ میں تمہارے لئے فروخت کروں اس کا تقاضا یہ ہے کہ اس کپڑے کو خاص خالد کے لئے بیچا جائے۔اس کی صورت یہ ہوگی کہ خالد کے حکم سے اس کا یہ کپڑا بیچ ڈالے۔ کیونکہ بیچ ایسی چیز ہے کہ اس میں نیابت جاری ہوتی ہے (یعنی ایک آدمی دوسرے آدمی کی طرف سے بیچ سکتا ہے مگر یہ بات نہیں پائی گئی۔ بخلاف اس کے کہ اگر یوں کہا ہو کہ اگر میں نے تمہارا یہ کپڑا بیچا تو ایسی صورت میں حانث ہو جائے گا۔ کیونکہ اس نے خالد کی ملکیت کا کپڑا بیچ ڈالا ہے اس لئے حانث ہوا خواہ خود اس کے حکم سے ہو یا حکم کے بغیر ہو۔ اور خواہ زید یہ بات جانتا ہو یا نہ جانتا ہو۔ کیونکہ اس نے اپنے پورے جملے میں جو تیرا کپڑا کہا ہے اس سے یہ کپڑا خالد کا ہی ہونا چاہئے۔ اس طرح سے کہ کپڑا اس کا مملوک
And whoever said to another, 'If I sell this cloth for you, then my wife is divorced,' and the one addressed [mahloof 'alayhi] mixed a cloth into the swearer's clothes, and he sold it without knowing, he does not become hanith. Because the particle 'lam' entered upon the sale, thus requiring its specification to him; and that is by his doing it by his [the other's] order, since sale can be done by proxy, and that was not found. Contrary to the case where he said, 'If I sell your cloth,' where he becomes hanith if he sells a cloth owned by him, whether by his order or without his order, whether he knew or did not know. Because the particle 'lam' entered upon the 'ayn (item), as it is closer to it, thus requiring specification of the item to him, and that is by it being owned by him. Analogous to this is goldsmithing and tailoring and everything that can be done by proxy. Unlike eating, drinking, and beating the slave, because these do not admit proxy, so the ruling does not differ in the two expressions. Translation: If Zayd said to Khalid, 'If I sell this cloth for you, my wife is divorced,' then Khalid hid that cloth among Zayd's clothes, and Zayd sold all the clothes without knowing that cloth was among them, then he does not become hanith. Because his statement 'I will sell for you' requires that the cloth be sold specifically for Khalid. This form would be that he sells Khalid's cloth by his order. Because sale is an act that can be done by proxy (i.e., one person can sell on behalf of another). But this was not present. Contrary to if he said, 'If I sell your cloth' – in that case he becomes hanith if he sells a cloth owned by Khalid, regardless of whether it was by his order or not, whether Zayd knew or did not know. Because in his entire sentence, he said 'your cloth', so this cloth must belong to Khalid, i.e., the cloth is his property.
Source pages p. 114

Commentary
This passage presents a ruling with a linguistic analysis of the particle 'lam'. It distinguishes between two oath formulations. Form 1: 'If I sell this cloth for you (li-ka)' – here 'lam' is attached to the verb 'sell', indicating that the sale is for the benefit of the addressee, which requires that the sale be done at his order, since sale admits proxy. If the cloth is sold without the addressee's order (even unknowingly), the oath is not broken. Form 2: 'If I sell your cloth (thawban laka)' – here 'lam' is attached to the noun 'cloth' (being closer), indicating ownership by the addressee. In this case, selling the cloth owned by the addressee breaks the oath regardless of order or knowledge. The passage also notes that analogous acts like goldsmithing and tailoring (where proxy is possible) follow the same rule, while acts like eating, drinking, and beating a slave (which cannot be done by proxy) do not differ between the two formulations. This shows the importance of grammatical analysis in Hanafi fiqh.

Key Terms
حرف اللام (ḥarf al-lām): The particle 'lam' (لِ) that indicates either agency ('for you' meaning by your order) or ownership ('your' meaning belonging to you).
نیابت (niyābah): Proxyship; the ability to do an act through a representative.
بیع (bayʿ): Sale; a contract that can be performed by proxy.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a person says, 'If I sell this cloth for you (li-ka), my wife is divorced,' and then sells the cloth without the addressee's order, what is the ruling?
A) He becomes hanith because the cloth was owned by the addressee.
B) He does not become hanith because the 'lam' indicates agency requiring the addressee's order.
C) He becomes hanith regardless because he sold the cloth.
D) He becomes hanith only if he knew the cloth belonged to the addressee.

Key Takeaway
The particle 'lam' in oaths indicates agency when attached to the verb and ownership when attached to the noun.

Oath Not to Beat Child vs. Slave: Agency and Attribution
Ruling on oath not to beat child then ordering another to beat

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why ordering another to beat one's child does not break an oath not to beat the child, and contrast this with the ruling for beating a slave.

Core Idea
An oath is broken only if the forbidden act is attributed to the swearer; attribution depends on the primary beneficiary of the act.

How can you order someone else to do what you swore you would not do? The Hanafi jurists draw a subtle distinction based on who benefits from the act. If the primary benefit goes to the person acted upon (the child's discipline), the act is not attributed to you, so your oath remains intact. But if the benefit goes to the master (slave's obedience), the act is attributed to you and you break your oath. This lesson unpacks the rule of attribution in oaths.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and ruling
A person swears not to beat his child, then orders another person to beat the child. According to the text, he is not hanith (breaker of the oath).
The ruling is that the oath is not broken.
2
Locate the dalil (proof)
The proof is a talil (reason): 'because the benefit of beating the child returns to the child himself' (discipline).
The reasoning is based on benefit attribution.
3
Understand the illa (effective cause)
The illa is that the benefit of the act goes to the child, not the father. Therefore, the act is not attributed to the father.
Attribution is key.
4
Compare with the case of a slave
In contrast, if a master orders someone to beat his slave, the benefit (ensuring obedience) goes to the master, so the act is attributed to the master and he would be hanith.
The distinction is based on who ultimately benefits.
5
Extract the general principle
The principle: In oaths, an act is attributed to the person who receives the primary benefit, not merely the one who gives the order.
This guides rulings in similar contexts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اگر کسی نے قسم اٹھائی کہ اپنے بچے کو نہیں مارے گا پھر دوسرے آدمی کو مارنے کا حکم دیا، اس نے مارا تو حانث نہیں ہوگا
وَمَنْ حَلَفَ لَا يَضْرِبْ وَلَدَه فَاَمَرَ انْسَانًا فَضَرَبَةِ لَمْ يَحْنِتُ فِي يَمِينِهِ لِأَنَّ مَنْفَعَةَ ضَرْبِ الْوَلَدِ عَائِدَةٌ إِلَيْهِ وَهُوَ الصَّابُ وَالتَّشَقَّفُ فَلَمْ يُنسَبْ فِعْلَهُ إِلَى الْامِرِ بِخِلَافِ الْأَمْرِ بِضَرْبِ الْعَبْدِلَانَّ مَنْفَعَتَهُ الْإِيْتِمَارُ بِأَمْرِهِ فَيُضَافُ الْفِعْلُ إِلَيْهِ
ترجمہ اور اگر کسی نے یہ قسم کھائی کہ میں اپنے لڑکے کو نہیں ماروں گا۔ پھر اس نے اپنے کسی آدمی کو حکم دیا اور اس نے اسے مارا تو یہ اپنی قسم میں حانث نہیں ہوگا۔ کیونکہ بچہ کے مارنے کا نتیجہ اور نفع خود بچہ کو پہنچتا ہے۔ یعنی وہ ادب سیکھتا ہے۔ اور راہ راست پر آ جاتا ہے۔ اس لئے اس کے نائب کا فعل اس کے حکم دینے والے کی طرف منسوب نہ ہو گا۔ بخلاف اس کے اگر غلام کو مارنے کے لئے کسی کو حکم دیا تو اس نائب کا مارنا اس مسئلہ میں خود اس حکم دہندہ کے مارنے کے حکم میں ہوگا۔ کیونکہ اس مار کا فائدہ یہ ہوگا کہ وہ غلام اپنے مولیٰ کے حکم کے خلاف کرنے سے خائف ہوگا۔ اور اس کی فرماں برداری کی کوشش کرے گا۔ اس لئے مارنے کا فعل اس کے مولیٰ کی طرف منسوب ہوگا۔
If someone swore that he would not beat his child, and then ordered another person to beat him, and he beat him, he will not be considered breaking his oath. Because the benefit of beating the child returns to the child himself, which is discipline and correction, so the act of the agent is not attributed to the commander. Unlike the order to beat a slave, because its benefit is that the slave complies with his master's command, so the act is attributed to him.
Source pages p. 114

Commentary
This passage presents a ruling concerning an oath about beating one's child. The author states that if a person swears not to beat his child, but then orders another person to beat the child, the act is not attributed to the oath-taker, so he does not violate his oath. The reason is that the benefit of beating a child (discipline) goes to the child himself, not to the father, so the beating is not considered the father's action. In contrast, if a master orders someone to beat his slave, the beating is attributed to the master because its benefit (ensuring obedience) goes to the master. The key distinction is whether the benefit of the act returns to the person who ordered it or to the one acted upon.

Key Terms
حانث (hānith): A person who breaks an oath by doing what he swore not to do, or failing to do what he swore to do.
مولیٰ (mawlā): Master of a slave; the owner.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A man swears he will not beat his son. He orders a friend to beat the son. According to the Hanafi ruling, is the man hanith?
A) Yes, because he caused the beating.
B) No, because the benefit of beating the son returns to the son, not the father.
C) Yes, because the father ordered it.
D) No, because beating a child is always permissible.

Key Takeaway
In oaths, the act is attributed to the person who primarily benefits from it, not merely the one who orders it.
